<leme no="545">

<page sig="a2r">
<section type="Title-page">
<heading>
A
COLLECTION
OF
English WORDS,

Not Generally used, with their
Significations and Original, in two
Alphabetical Catalogues,

The one of such as are proper to
the <f type="i">Northern</f>, the other to the
<f type="i">Southern Counties</f>.

With <f type="i">Catalogues</f> of <f type="i">English Birds</f>
and <f type="i">Fishes</f>: And an Account of the
preparing and refining such Metals
and Minerals as are gotten
in <f type="i">England</f>.
</heading>

By <f type="i">J##sp##O##sp##H##sp##N R##sp##A##sp##Y</f> Fellow of the
R##sp##O##sp##Y##sp##A##sp##L S##sp##O##sp##C##sp##I##sp##E##sp##T##sp##Y.

<f type="i">L##sp##O##sp##N##sp##D##sp##O##sp##N</f>,
Printed by <f type="i">H. Bruges</f> for <f type="i">Tho. Burrell</f> at the
<f type="i">Golden-Ball</f> under St. <f type="i">Dunstans Church</f> in
<f type="i">Fleetstreet</f>, 1674.
</section>
</page>

<page sig="a2v">
</page>

<page sig="a3r">
<section type="Dedication">
TO HIS

Honoured Friend

<f type="i">P##sp##E##sp##T##sp##E##sp##R C##sp##O##sp##U##sp##R##sp##T##sp##H##sp##O##sp##P##sp##E</f>

OF

<f type="i">Danny</f> in <f type="i">Sussex</f> Esquire.

SIR,

<f type="bk">T</f><f type="i">Hough I need no o&shy;
ther Motive to in&shy;
duce me to present
you with this Col&shy;
lection of English
Words, but that I
might take occasion publickly to own
my Obligations to you, as well for
your long continued Friendship, as
</page>

<page sig="a3v">
for the Assistance you have some&shy;
time afforded me in those Studies to
which I am I think naturally in&shy;
clined, yet one Circumstance did
more especially lead me to make
choice of you for its Patron; and
that is, that you were the first that
Contributed to it, and indeed the
Person that put me upon it; and
so it being in good measure your
own, I have reason to hope that you
will Favourably accept it. I
confess the Work is so inconside&shy;
rable, that I am somewhat ashamed
to prefix your name before it, but
having nothing else left of my own,
which I design to trouble the World
with, as not knowing whether I may
live so long as to perfect my intended
Method and History of Plants; I
</page>

<page sig="a4r">
chuse rather to present you with this,
then lose the Honour of being known to
have such a Friend, or neglect the duty
of making acknowledgments where
they are due, especially having already
made Presents of this Nature to others
of my Friends, which is enough to ex&shy;
cuse this Dedication intended to no o&shy;
ther purposes by</f>

Sir,

Your very humble Servant,

<f type="i">JO. RAY</f>.
</section>
</page>

<page sig="a4v">
</page>

<page sig="a5r">
<section type="To The Reader">
<heading>
TO THE

R##sp##E##sp##A##sp##D##sp##E##sp##R.
</heading>

<f type="bk">I</f>N my travels through seve&shy;
ral parts of <f type="i">England</f>, besides
other things, which I princi&shy;
pally minded and pursued, I
could not but take notice of the
difference of Dialect, and variety
of Local words (for so I will take
leave to call such as are not of
general use) in divers Counties,
by Reason whereof in many
places, especially of the North,
the Language of the common
people, is to a stranger very dif&shy;
ficult to be understood. Where&shy;
</page>

<page sig="a5v">
upon I thought it might be worth
the while to make a Collection
of such words for my own use,
and began first to set down those
that occurred to me in common
discourse. But making short
stayes in particular places, and
conversing but with few persons,
I found that what I could take
notice of my <emend err="felf">self</emend> would be but
an inconsiderable part of what
were in use among the vulgar.

Therefore I desired my friends
and acquaintance living in se&shy;
veral Countreys to communicate
to me what they had observed
each of their own Countrey
words, or should afterwards ga&shy;
ther up out of the mouths of the
people; which divers of them
</page>

<page sig="a6r">
accordingly did. To whose
contributions I must acknow&shy;
ledge my self to owe the greatest
part of the words, I now present
the <f type="i">Reader</f> with, in these Cata&shy;
logues. The considerations
which induced me to make them
publick were. <f type="i">First</f>, because I
knew not of anything that hath
been already done in this kind.
2. Because I conceive, they may
be of some use to them who
shall have occasion to travel the
Northern Counties, in helping
them to understand the common
language there. 3. Because they
may also afford some diversion
to the curious, and give them
occasion of making many con&shy;
siderable remarks.
</page>

<page sig="a6v">
As for the sence and import
of these words, I am confident
I am not therein mistaken, ha&shy;
ving received it from persons
that well understand the force
and meaning of them in the
places where they are used. But
of their original or Etymology,
for want of sufficient skill in the
<f type="i">Saxon, Dutch</f> and <f type="i">Danish</f> Lan&shy;
guages, I have not been able to
give so good an account as I de&shy;
sired, and by those helps might
have done. For what I have per&shy;
formed in this kind, I acknow&shy;
ledge my self to have been for
the most part beholden to Dr.
<f type="i">Skinner's <term lang="la">Etymologicon Lingu&aelig; An&shy;
glican&aelig;</f></term>, and Mr. <f type="i">Somners Saxon
Dictionary</f>, by turning over
</page>

<page sig="a7r">
which last work I find a great
part of these words to be pure
Saxon, or manifestly derived
thence; and I doubt not but
many of the rest, which occur
not in that Dictionary (which
being collected out of but a few
<f type="i">Books</f> and fragments that remain
of that Language, cannot be con&shy;
ceived to comprehend near all
the words in common use) are
of like Original.

I am sensible that this Col&shy;
lection is far from Perfect, not
conteining perchance more then
one moiety of the Local words
used in all the several Counties
of <f type="i">England</f>. But it is as full as
I can at present easily make it,
and may give occasion to the
</page>

<page sig="a7v">
curious in each <f type="i">Countrey</f> to sup&shy;
ply what are wanting, and so
make the work compleat.

<wordgroup1 type="u">
I have added hereto, 1. a Ca&shy;
talogue of <f type="i">English Birds</f>, as well
such as continually abide and
breed with us; as those they call
Birds of passage, that come
and go at certain seasons, ad&shy;
mitting also some which fre&shy;
quent our coasts only upon oc&shy;
casion of hard Winters or other
accidents, all which either my
Self or Mr. <f type="i">Francis Willughby</f>
have both seen and described.
Since this Catalogue was sent
away to the Press, among some
Pictures of Birds which I have
received from the Learned and
deservedly Famous Sr. <f type="i">Thomas
</page>

<page sig="a8r">
Brown</f> of <f type="i">Norwich</f>, I find two or
three <f type="i">English</f> Birds by me omit&shy;
ted, as whereof I was not then
certain, 1. <term lang="la"><f type="i">Oedicnemus</f> Bellonii</term>,
by him also first observed in
<f type="i">England</f>. <wordentry type="g"><form lexeme="stone curlew(n)">Upon the Picture of
it sent me by Sr. <f type="i">Tho</f>. I find
inscribed, <f type="i">A stone Curlew, from
about</f> Thetford,</form> <xpln><f type="i">whereabout they
breed. It hath a Remarkeable eye
and note somewhat like a green plo&shy;
ver</f>,</xpln></wordentry>
<wordentry type="g"><form lexeme="shearwater(n)">2. <f type="i">A shear-water</f></form> <xpln>inscribed,
<f type="i"><term lang="la">Larus niger, pectore albido, rostro
adunco</term></f>,</xpln></wordentry> <wordentry type="g"><form lexeme="barker(n)">3. The <f type="i">Barker</f>,</form> <xpln lexeme="marsh bird(n)">a marsh
Bird with a long bill, to which
there was no <f type="i">Latine</f> name added,</xpln><lemenote>Not found in OED except possibly at "barker," n. 1, 3.</lemenote></wordentry>
<wordentry type="g"><form>4. <f type="i"><term lang="la">Mergulos melanoleucos rostro
acuto brevi</term></f>,</form> <xpln lexeme="silerella(n)">5. A little Bird of
a tawney colour on the back, and
a blew head, yellow bill, black
</page>

<page sig="a8v">
legs, shot in an Osiar yard, cal&shy;
led by Sir <f type="i">Tho</f>. for distinction
sake <term lang="la">S<f type="i">ilerella</f></term>.</xpln><lemenote>Not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

2. Two Catalogues of fishes,
one of all our <f type="i">English</f> fresh
water fishes; the other of such
sea-fish as are taken about <f type="i">Pen&shy;
sans</f> in <f type="i">Carnwal</f>.

3. Several relations of the
manner of smelting, refining
and preparing such metals and
minerals as are found in <f type="i">England</f>,
according to the best informa&shy;
tion I could get in the places
where they are wrought.
</section>
</page>

<page sig="b1r">
<section type="Glossary 1: North and South Countries">
<heading>
A

Collection
OF
LOCAL WORDS
PROPER
To the <f type="i">North</f> and <f type="i">South</f>
COUNTREYS.
</heading>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="a"><alpha>A</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="addle(v)"><f type="bk">T</f>O <f type="i">Adle</f> or <f type="i">Addle</f>;</form> <xpln lexeme="earn(v)" lexeme="reward(n)" lexeme="recompense(n)" lexeme="requital(n)">to
Earn, from the ancient
Saxon word <f type="i"><etym lang="oe">Ed-lean</etym></f>, a
reward, recompence or
requital.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="ch" lexeme="agate(adv)"><f type="i">Agate</f>; Ches.</form> <xpln lexeme="just going(adv)" lexeme="at the way(n)" lexeme="upon the way(n)">Just
going, as <f type="i">I am Agate.
Gate</f> in the Northern
Dialect signifies a way, so that <emend err="a"></emend> Agate is at
or upon the way.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="b1v">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="amel(pp)"><f type="i">A mell</f>;</form> <xpln lexeme="among(pp)" lexeme="betwixt(pp)" lexeme="a midle(pp)">Among, betwixt, contracted from
a midle; Some pronounce it <f type="i">ameld</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="a'nauntrins(adv)"><f type="i">Anauntrins</f>;</form> <xpln lexeme="if so be(adv)" lexeme="if peradventure(adv)">If so be. I know not what
the Original of this should be, unless it be
from <f type="i">An</f>, for <f type="i">if</f>, and <f type="i">Auntrins</f> contracted
from Peradventure.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="sc" lexeme="anent(pp)"><f type="i">Anent</f>;</form> <xpln lexeme="over against(pp)" lexeme="concerning(pp)">Over against, concerning, A word
of frequent use among the <f type="i">Scots</f>. Some de&shy;
duce it from the Greek <etym lang="gk">&#x03b5;&#x03bd;&#x03b1;&#x03bd;&#x03c0; &#x03b5;&#x03bd;&#x03b1;&#x03bd;&#x03c4;&#x03af;&#x03bf;&#x03bd;</etym> <f type="i"><term lang="la">Op&shy;
positum</f>. Nec male sane (inquit Skinnerus in
<f type="i">Etymologico Lingu&aelig; Anglican&aelig;)</f> si vel soni
vel sensus convenientiam respicias. Sed quo
commercio Gr&aelig;ci Scotis totius Europ&aelig;
longitudine difficilis vocabula impertiri potu&shy;
erurit? Mallem igitur deducere ab AS</term> <etym lang="oe"><f type="i">Nean</f></etym>
<term lang="la">Prop&eacute;, addit&acirc; particul&agrave; initiali otiosa AS</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="nt" lexeme="arain(n)">An <f type="i">Arain</f>:</form> <xpln lexeme="spider(n)" lexeme="spider, large(n)">a Spider. <etym lang="la"><f type="i">a</f> Lat. <f type="i">Aranea</f></etym>, it is used
only for the larger kind of Spiders. <f type="i">Not&shy;
tinghamshire</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="ark(n)"><f type="i">An Ark</f>;</form> <xpln lexeme="chest(n)" lexeme="buttery(n)">A large Chest to put corn or
fruit in like the Bing of a Buttery; from the
Latin word <f type="i"><emend err="Arca" source="1691"><etym lang="la">Archa</etym></emend></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="arles(n)"><f type="i">Arles</f> or <f type="i">Earles</f>;</form> <xpln lexeme="earnest(n)" lexeme="arles-penny(n)" lexeme="earnest-penny(n)">Earnest, An <f type="i">Arles-penny</f>,
An Earnest penny, from the Latin word <f type="i"><emend err="archa" source="errata"><etym lang="la">Arrha</etym></emend></f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="asker(n)">An <f type="i">Asker</f>;</form> <xpln lexeme="newt(n)" lexeme="eft(n)">a <f type="i">Newt</f>, or <f type="i">Eft</f>, <term lang="la">Salamandra
aquatica</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="astite(adv)" lexeme="as tite(adv)">2. <f type="i">Astite</f>;</form> <xpln lexeme="anon(adv)" lexeme="shortly(adv)" lexeme="as soon(adv)" lexeme="as tide(adv)" lexeme="tide(n)" lexeme="soon(adv)" lexeme="tider(adv)" lexeme="titter(adv)" lexeme="sooner(adv)" lexeme="Shrovetide(pn)" lexeme="-tide(n)" lexeme="Whitsuntide(pn)">Anon, shortly, or As soon.
i. e. <f type="i">As Tide</f>, <f type="i">Tide</f> in the North signifies
soon, and <f type="i">tider</f> or <f type="i">titter</f> sooner. The tider
(that is the sooner) you come the tider you'll
</page>

<page sig="b2r">
goe; from the Saxon <f type="i"><etym lang="oe">Tid</etym></f> signifying time,
which is still in use, as in Shrove-tide, Whit&shy;
sun-tide, &c.</xpln><lemenote>"as tide" not found in OED as adverb.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="easily(adv)">1. As <f type="i">Asly</f>;</form> <xpln lexeme="willingly(adv)">As willingly.</xpln><lemenote>"asly" not found as OED variant for "easily."</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="cu" lexeme="attercop(n)">An <f type="i"><emend err="Attercob" source="1691">Attercop</emend></f>;</form> <xpln lexeme="spider(n)">A <emend err="Spiders Web" source="1691">Spider</emend>. <f type="i">Cum&shy;
berl</f>.</xpln></wordentry>


<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="average(n)"><f type="i">Average</f>;</form> <xpln lexeme="breaking of fields(n)" lexeme="eddish(n)" lexeme="roughings(n)" lexeme="beast(n)" lexeme="beast, labouring(n)" lexeme="good(n)" lexeme="chattel(n)" lexeme="have(v)" lexeme="possess(v)" lexeme="oat(n)" lexeme="feeding for cattle(n)" lexeme="pasturage(n)">The breaking of corn fields;
Eddish, Roughings. <f type="i">Average</f> in Law signifies
either the beasts which tenants and vassals
were to provide their Lords for certain ser&shy;
vices, or that money that was laid out by
Merchants to repair the losses suffered by
Shipwrack; and so it is deduced from the
old word <f type="i">Aver</f> [<etym lang="la">Averium</etym>] signifying a
labouring beast: or <etym lang="la"><f type="i">Averia</f></etym> signifying Goods
or Chattels, from the French <f type="i"><etym lang="fr">Avoir</etym></f> to have
or possess. But in the sence we have used it,
it may possibly come from <f type="i"><etym lang="ld">Haver</etym></f> signifying
Oates; or from <f type="i"><etym lang="la">Averia</etym></f>, beasts, being as
much as feeding for cattal, pasturage.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="ambry(n)" lexeme="aumbry(n)">An <f type="i">Aumbry</f> or <f type="i">Ambry</f> or <f type="i">Aumery</f>.</form> <xpln lexeme="pantry(n)" lexeme="cupboard(n)" lexeme="head(n)" lexeme="cupboard head(n)" lexeme="side-table(n)">A
Pantry or Cupboard to set victuals in:
<f type="i">Skinner</f> makes it to Signify a Cupboards
head, or side-table: <term lang="la">super quam vasa men&shy;
saria & tota argentea supellex ad usum con&shy;
vivioram exponitur: a Fr. G.</term> <etym lang="fr"><f type="i">Aumoire</f>,
<f type="i">Armaire & Armoire</f></etym>, It. <f type="i"><etym lang="it">Armaro</etym></f> <term lang="la">idem fig&shy;
nantibus, q. d. Latine <f type="i">Armarium</f></term>. Prov.
No sooner up, but the head in the Aumbry,
</page>

<page sig="b2v">
and nose in the cup; in which sentence it must
needs signifie a Cup-board for Victuals.</xpln><lemenote>"cupboard head" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="aunters(adv)"><f type="i">Aunters</f>:</form> <xpln lexeme="peradventure(adv)" lexeme="chance(v)">Peradventure, or, in case, if it
chance. I guess it to be contracted from
Adventure, which was first mollified into
Auventure, and then easily contracted into
<f type="i">Aunter</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="undern(n)">The <f type="i">Aunder</f>, or as they pronounce it
in Cheshire <term lang="ch"><f type="i">Oneder</f></term>;</form> <xpln lexeme="afternoon(n)">The afternoon.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="b"><alpha>B.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="bain(a)"><f type="bk">B</f><f type="i">Ain</f>;</form> <xpln lexeme="willing(a)" lexeme="forward(a)">Willing, Forward: opposed to
Lither.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="balk(n)">The <f type="i">Balk</f> or <f type="i">Bawk</f>:</form> <xpln lexeme="summer-beam(n)" lexeme="balk(n)" lexeme="pole, roof(n)">The Summer-beam.
<f type="i">Balks</f>, <f type="i">Bawks</f>: Poles laid over a stable or
other building for the roof, <etym lang="la">&agrave; Belgico &
Teuton.</etym> <etym lang="bl"><f type="i">Balk</f></etym>, <term lang="la">Trabs, tignum</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="balk-staff(n)"> A <f type="i">Balk staffe</f>:</form> <xpln lexeme="quarter-staff(n)" lexeme="staff(n)" lexeme="pole(n)" lexeme="beam(n)">A Quarter-staffe, A great
staffe like a Pole or Beam.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="bannock(n)">A <f type="i">Bannock</f>,</form> <xpln lexeme="oat-cake(n)" lexeme="oaten bread(n)" lexeme="tharf-cake(n)" lexeme="clap-bread(n)" lexeme="kitchiness-bread(n)" lexeme="riddle-bread, -cake(n)" lexeme="hand-hoven bread(n)" lexeme="jannock(n)">An <f type="i">Oat-cake</f> kneaded with
water only and baked in the Embers. In
Lancashire, and other parts of the North,
they make several sorts of Oaten bread, which
they call by several names: as 1. <f type="i">Thar&shy;
cakes</f>, the same with <f type="i">Bannocks</f>, viz. Cakes
made of Oat-meal as it comes from the mill
and fair water, without Yeast or leaven, and
</page>

<page sig="b2r">
so baked. 2. <f type="i">Clap-bread</f>: Thin hard Oat&shy;
cakes. 3. <f type="i">Kitchiness-bread</f>: Thin soft Oat&shy;
cakes made of thin batter. 4. <f type="i">Riddle-cakes</f>:
thick Sour-cakes, from which differs little
that which they call <f type="i">Hand-hoven Bread</f>,
having but little leaven, and being kneaded
stiffer. 5. <f type="i">Jannock</f>, Oaten bread made up
in loaves.</xpln><lemenote>"kitchiness-bread" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="yk" lexeme="bargh(n)">A <f type="i">Bargh</f>,</form> <xpln lexeme="horseway(n)">A Horseway up a steep hill.
<f type="i">York-shire</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="bairn(n)">A <f type="i"><etym lang="oe">Barn</etym></f> or <f type="i"><etym lang="oe">Bearn</etym></f>:</form> <xpln lexeme="child(n)" lexeme="son(n)">A Child: it is an an&shy;
cient Saxon word. In the ancient Teutonick
<etym lang="tt"><f type="i">Barn</f></etym> signifies a son, derived perchance from
the Syriack <f type="i"><etym lang="sy">Bar</etym></f>, <etym lang="la">Filius</etym>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="bairn(n)" lexeme="team(n)" lexeme="bairn-team(n)"><f type="i">Bearn-teams</f>,</form> <xpln lexeme="brood(n)" lexeme="children(n)" lexeme="issue(n)" lexeme="offspring(n)">Broods of Children, as they
expounded it to me. I find that <f type="i"><etym lang="oe">Bearn-team</etym></f>
in the Saxon, signifies Issue, offspring, Chil&shy;
dren, from <f type="i"><etym lang="oe">team</etym></f>, <etym lang="la">soboles</etym>, and <etym lang="oe">Bearn</etym>.</xpln><lemenote>"bearn-team" not found in OED.</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="yk" lexeme="bearing(n)" lexeme="with child(n)"><f type="i">Bearing</f> with Child:</form> <xpln lexeme="breeding(n)">Breeding, <etym lang="la">gravid</etym>.
<f type="i">York-shire</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="beck(n)">A <f type="i"><etym lang="oe">Beck</etym></f>:</form> <xpln lexeme="brook(n)">a small brook: a word common
to the ancient Saxon, High and low Dutch
and Danish.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="birr(n)"><f type="i">Beer</f> or <f type="i">Birre</f> q. Beare: </form> <xpln lexeme="force(n)" lexeme="might(n)">Force, might.
<term lang="ch">Withaw my beer</term>. <f type="i">Chesh</f>. i. e. with all my
force.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="ch" lexeme="bight(n)"><f type="i">Beight</f> of the Elbow: </form> <xpln lexeme="bending(n)">Bending of the
Elbow. <f type="i">Chesh</f>. <emend err="As">A s</emend>ubstantive from the pre&shy;
terperfect tense of <f type="i">Bend</f>, as <f type="i">Bought</f> of the
like signification, from <f type="i">Bow</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="b3v">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="belive(adv)"><f type="i">Belive</f>:</form> <xpln lexeme="anon(adv)" lexeme="by and by(adv)" lexeme="toward night(adv)" lexeme="by the eve(adv)">Anon, by and by, or towards

The French call a spirit appearing by night
or a Ghost, a <f type="i">Fantosme</f>, from Phantasma,

that hath as little bulk or solidity in it as a

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="yode, yede(v)"><f type="i">Yewd</f> or <f type="i">Yod</f>:</form> <xpln lexeme="go(v)" lexeme="yewing(n)" lexeme="going(n)">Went, <f type="i">Yewing</f>: Going,

sensu</term>. <f type="i">Spencer</f> also in his Fairie Queen,

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="alp(n)" lexeme="nope(n)">A <f type="bk">A</f><f type="i">Lp</f> or <f type="i">Nope</f>:</form> <xpln lexeme="bullfinch(n)">a Bulfinch. I first
took notice of this word in <f type="i">Suf&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="ss" lexeme="amper(n)">An <f type="i"><etym lang="ex">Amper</etym></f>:</form> <xpln lexeme="flaw in cloth(n)" lexeme="fault in cloth(n)" lexeme="tumour(n)" lexeme="rising(n)" lexeme="pustule(n)">a fault or flaw in linnen or

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="ex" lexeme="yepsen(n)" lexeme="gowpen(n)">A <f type="i">Yaspen</f> <term lang="en">or</term> <f type="i">Yeepsen</f>:</form> <xpln>in Essex signifies as
much as can be taken up in both hands <emend err="joy n'd">joyn'd</emend>
together. <f type="i">Gouldman</f> renders it <term lang="la">vola seu mani&shy;
pulus, fortean a nostro.</term> <etym lang="en"><f type="i">Grasping</f></etym>, <term lang="la">elisa propter
euphoniam litera canina <f type="i">r</f>, and <f type="i">g</f>, in <f type="i">y</f> facillima
san&egrave; & vulgatissima nostr&aelig; lingu&aelig; mutatione
transeunte: q. d. quantum quis vola compre&shy;
hendere potest</term>, <f type="i">Skinner</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

In <f type="i">Sussex</f> for hasp, clasp, wasp, they pro&shy;
nounce hapse, clapse, wapse, &c. for neck
nick, for throat throtte, for choak, chock.
Set'n down, let'n stand, come again and fet'n
anon. <term lang="dv">C'have cat so much c'ham quit a
quot</term>, <f type="i">Devon</f>. i. e. I ean eat no more, I have
eat so much that I am cloyed.
</wordgroup1>

<page sig="g2r">
<section type="Glossary 3: English Birds">

<heading>
A
CATALOGUE:
OF
ENGLISH BIRDS
As well such as constantly abide
and breed in <f type="i">England</f>, as those that
come and go at certain Seasons.
</heading>

<wordgroup1 type="t" object="Rapacious Diurnal Birds"><alpha>Rapacious Diurnal Birds.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la"><f type="bk">A</f><f type="i">Quila</f>:</form> <xpln lexeme="eagle(n)">The Eagle; What sort I
cannot certainly determine. But
that there have been several seen, and
some bred in E<f type="i">ngland</f> I am well
assured. Particularly <f type="i">Anno</f> 1668. in the
Woodlands near <f type="i">Derwent</f> in the Peak of
<f type="i">Derbyshire</f> was found an Eagles nest, made
of great sticks resting the one end upon the
ledge of a Rock, the other upon two Birch
trees; above the sticks was a layer of rushes
</page>

<page sig="g2v">
upon the Rushes a layer of Heath, and upon
<emend err="he">the</emend> Heath Rushes again; upon which lay
one young one and an addle Egge, and by
them a lamb and a hare, and three heath&shy;
poults. The nest was about two yards
square, and had no hollow in it: The young
Eagle was as black as a Hobby, of the shape
of a Gos-hawk, of almost the weight of a
Goose, feathered down to the foot, and ha&shy;
ving a ring of white about the tail, an <f type="i"><term lang="la">Pygar&shy;
gus</term></f> Aldr. and it is said that there are Eagles
build yearly on the Rocks of <f type="i">Snowdon</f> in
<f type="i">Carnarvanshire</f> in <f type="i">Wales</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="sparrow-hawk(n)">The <f type="i">Sparrow-hawk</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Accipiter fringilla&shy;
rius, Recentioribus, Nisus</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="buzzard, bald(n)">The <f type="i">Bald Buzzard</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Balbusardus Anglo&shy;
rum, Haliaetus</term>, <f type="i">Aldrov</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="gull, great gray(n)" lexeme="wagel(n)">The great Gray Gull</f>: an <f type="i">Wagel</f></form> <xpln><term lang="la">Cornubi&shy;
ensium</term>?
Of this Bird the Cornish men about <f type="i">Pen&shy;
sans</f> report, that he pursues and strikes at the
small Gull so long, till out of fear it mutes;
The <f type="i">Wagell</f> presently follows and greedily
devours the excrement, catching it sometimes
before it be fallen to the water. This seve&shy;
ral Seamen affirmed themselves to have often&shy;
times seen.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="gull, great black and white(n)"><f type="i">The great black and white Gull</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Larus
maximus ex albo & nigro varius</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="gannet(n)"><f type="i">The Gannet</f>,</form> <xpln lexeme="cataract(n)" lexeme="pilchard(n)">An Catarractes. We saw
about St. <f type="i">Ives</f> in <f type="i">Cornwal</f> many of these
birds flying. It hath long wings, and a long
neck, and flyeth strongly, it prys upon Pil&shy;
chards; the Scoles whereof great numbers of
this fowl constantly frequent and pursue.
</page>

<page sig="g8v">
It casts it self down with great violence upon
its prey, insomuch that they say one way to
catch it is by fastning a Pilchard to a deal
board a little under water, upon which it will
precipitate it self with such vehemency, that
it will dash out its own brains against the
board.</xpln><lemenote>"cataract" (bird) antedates earliest OED citation (1868).</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="coddy-moddy(n)" lexeme="gull, lesser grey(n)"><f type="i">The Coddy moddy</f> or lesser grey Gull:</form> <xpln>an

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="puffin(n)" lexeme="curviere(n)"><f type="i">The Puffin</form> <xpln> or Curviere</f>: <term lang="la">Puffinus An&shy;

<page sig="h2r">
<section type="Glossary 4: Fishes">
<heading>
A.
CATALOGUE
OF
FISHES

Taken about <f type="i">Pensans</f> and St.
<f type="i">Ives</f> in <f type="i">Cornwall</f> given us by
one of the Ancientest and most
experienced fishermen, the most
whereof we saw during our
stay there.
</heading>

<wordgroup1 type="t" object="Of the Cetaceous Kind"><alpha><f type="i">Of the Cetaceous Kind</f>.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="whale(n)"> 1. <f type="bk">T</f>He <f type="i">Whale</f>,</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Cetus, Bal&aelig;na</term>: of
what sort he could not tell us.
<term lang="la">Vulgus enim non distinguit</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="porpoise(n)"> 2. The <f type="i">Porpesse</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Phoc&aelig;na</term>
<f type="i">Rondel</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="h2v">
These Fishes have lungs and breathe like
quadrupeds, are also viviparous and give
suck to their young.
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="t" object="Of the Cartilagineous kind"><alpha><f type="i">Of the Cartilagineous kind,</alpha>
<wordgroup2 type="t" object="Long"><alpha><f type="i">Long.</f>.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="shark, blue(n)"> 3. <f type="i">Blew Sharks</f>;</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Glaucus</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="shark, white(n)"> 4. <f type="i">White Sharks</f>.</form><xpln></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="tope(n)"> 5. <f type="i">Topes</f>;</form> <xpln>An <term lang="la">Mustelus l&aelig;vis secundus seu
Canosa</term> S<f type="i">alviani</f>?</xpln><lemenote>Antedates earliest OED citation (1686).</lemenote></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="picked dog(n)"> 6. <f type="i">Picked Dogs</f>;</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Catulus spinax</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="rough-hound(n)"> 7. <f type="i">Rough Hounds</f>;</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Mustelus, an l&aelig;vis
primus</term> S<f type="i">alviani</f>?</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="morgy(n)"> 8. <f type="i">Morgay</f>;</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Catulus major</term> S<f type="i">alviani</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="t" object="Broad or Flat"><alpha><f type="i">Broad or Flat</f>.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="thornback(n)"> 9. <f type="i">Thornback</f>;</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Raia clavata</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="flair(n)" lexeme="ray(n)">10. <f type="i">Flair</f> or <f type="i">Ray</f>;</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Raia l&aelig;vis</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="monkfish(n)">11. <f type="i">Monk-fish</f>:</form> <xpln lexeme="skate(n)" lexeme="angel-fish(n)">which either is or ought
to be called S<f type="i">kate</f>, if we follow the Ety&shy;
mology of the word; <term lang="la">Squatina</term>. The Ita&shy;
lians call it <term lang="it">Pesce Angelo</term>, The Angel&shy;
Fish.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="h3r">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="piper(n)" lexeme="gurnard(n)">12. The <f type="i">Piper</f>,</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Raio-squatina</term> <f type="i">Rondel</f>.
I am not ignorant that the Cornish men call
another Fish, <f type="i">viz</f>. A sort of <term lang="la">Cuculus</term> or
Gurnard by the name of Piper: wherefore
this Homonymy is to be carefully noted to
avoid confusion.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form>13. <f type="i">Pesc mollan</f>;</form> <xpln lexeme="frog-fish(n)" lexeme="sea devil(n)">the Frog-fish, or Sea&shy;
Divel: <term lang="la">Rana piscatrix</term>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>
</wordgroup1>

<wordgroup1 type="t" object="Spinous or Bony Fishes"><alpha>Spinous or Bony Fishes.</alpha>
<wordgroup2 type="t" object="Flat-Fishes that swim sideways ..."><alpha><f type="i">Flat-Fishes that swim sideways and
lye most part grovelling at the
bottome</f>.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="halibut(n)">14. <f type="i">Holibut</f> or <f type="i">Halibut</f>;</form> <xpln>the biggest
of all this kind, an <term lang="la">Hippoglossus</term> <f type="i">Ron&shy;
del</f>?</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="turbot(n)" lexeme="bret(n)">15. <f type="i">Turbot</f> <term lang="la">Rhombus</term>.</form> <xpln>These names of
Turbot and Halibut are confounded in several
Countreys. What in the North they call
the Halibut in the North they call the Turbot;
and the Turbot the Bret, nay in some parts
of the <emend err="Weft" source ="errata">West</emend> of England they call the Tur&shy;
bot Bret and the Halibut Turbot.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="bib(n)" lexeme="blinds(n)">26. The <f type="i">Bib</f> or <f type="i">Blinds</f>.</form><xpln></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="bulcard(n)">27. The <f type="i">Bulcard</f>.</form><xpln></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="whiting(n)">28. The <f type="i">Whiting</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Asellus mollis</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

Of these fishes we saw and described all,
save the <f type="i">Bulcard</f>: several of them we judge
not yet described by any Authour extant in
print: indeed the writers of natural History
of Animals living far from the Ocean, and
so having never had opportunity of seeing
these kinds of Fishes, which are proper to the
Ocean and not found in the Mediterranean
Sea, write very confusedly and obscurely
concerning them.
To these may be referred two other Fishes,
which have not three fins on the back, because
their flesh is like that of the precedent, and
they are usually salted and dried in like man&shy;
ner, <f type="i">viz</f>.

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="ling(n)">29. <f type="i">Ling</f>:</form> <xpln>by some called <term lang="la">Asellus longus</term>,
and by some <term lang="la">Asellus Islandicus</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<page sig="h7r">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="phoca(n)" lexeme="soile(n)">On the Rocks near the Lands end they
often find the <f type="i"><term lang="la">Phoc&aelig;</term></f> (<f type="i">which they call <etym lang="co">Soils</etym></f>)
sleeping.</form> <xpln>Sometimes they kill them by strik&shy;
ing them cross the snout with a pole, other&shy;
whiles they shoot them. Some of these
<f type="i">Soils</f> (<f type="i">they say</f>) grow to the bigness of a
heifer of two years old, and they are of
divers Colours. They defend themselves by
casting stones backward with their feet upon
those that come near them. Being shot dead
the male they say sinks down presently to
the bottom of the water, but the female
flotes or swims: which is not to be credi&shy;
ted.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="seal(n)" lexeme="soile(n)">They distinguish between <f type="i">Soils</f> and <etym lang="co">S<f type="i">ieles</etym></f>:</form>
<xpln> the Siele they affirm to be a Fish and no <f type="i">Am&shy;
phibium</f>, much less then the soile, and not
taken upon our Coasts.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="char(n)" lexeme="char, red(n)">The <etym lang="wm"><f type="i">Charre</f></etym> of Winander-mere in West&shy;
morland:</form> <xpln lexeme="char, gilt(n)">This I take to be the same with
the <f type="i">Welsh <etym lang="we">Torgoch</etym></f>, taken in the lake of
Llanberis near <f type="i">Snowdon-hill</f>, and in other
pools in <f type="i">Carnarvanshire</f>. The same I saw
and described at <f type="i">Zug</f> in <f type="i">Switzerland</f>, by the
name of <f type="i"><etym lang="un">Reutell</etym></f>: which the fisher men, there,
said was proper to their lake. Of this fish
there are two sorts taken in <f type="i">Winander-mere</f>.
The greater having a red belly they call the
<etym lang="wm">red <f type="i">Charre</f></etym>: and the lesser having a white
belly, which they call the <etym lang="wm">Gilt or Gelt
Charre</etym>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="gwyniad(n)">The <etym lang="we"><f type="i">Guiniad</f></etym>:</form> <xpln lexeme="skelly(n)">found in the lake of <f type="i">Bala</f>
in <f type="i">Merionethshire</f> in <f type="i">Wales</f>. This is the same

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="trout(n)" lexeme="Lincolnshire shard(n)" lexeme="salmon trout(n)" lexeme="bull-trout(n)" lexeme="trout, grey(n)" lexeme="scurf(n)"><f type="bk">T</f>He <f type="i">Trout</f>;</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Trutta</term>. Of these there

<f type="i">Lincoln-shire <etym lang="li">Shard</etym></f>, the <f type="i">Salmon&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="samlet(n)">The <term lang="hr"><f type="i">Samlet</f></term>:</form> <xpln>a small Fish of the trout-kind
taken in <f type="i">Hereford</f> River.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="grayling(n)" lexeme="umber(n)">The <f type="i">Grayling</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Thymalus</term>. I take this to
be the same Fish, which in some places of the
North they call the <f type="i">Umber</f>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="pike(n)" lexeme="pickerel(n)" lexeme="Jack(n)">The <f type="i">Pike</f>, <f type="i">Pickrell</f> or <f type="i">Jack</f>:</form> <xpln>for these
are but several names of the same Fish accor&shy;
ding to its age or bigness, <term lang="la">Lucius</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="carp(n)">The <f type="i">Carp</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Cyprinus</term>. This Fish, though
now there is none more common with us,
was but lately brought over into <f type="i">England.
Leonard Mascall</f> in his book of Fishing
</page>

<page sig="i1r">
saith that he himself was the first that brought
in Carps and Pippins.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="bream(n)">The <f type="i">Bream</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Cyprnius latus</term>; Abra&shy;
mis.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="perch(n)" lexeme="bass(n)">The <f type="i">Perch</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Perca</term>, this Fish at <f type="i">Huls</f>
Water before mentioned they called the
<etym lang="cu"><f type="i">Basse</f></etym>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="ruff(n)">The <f type="i">Ruffe</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Perca aurata</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="tench(n)">The <f type="i">Tench</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Tinca</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="barbel(n)">The <f type="i">Barble</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Barbus</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="chub(n)" lexeme="chevin(n)">The <f type="i">Chub</f> or <f type="i">Chevin</f>: </form> <xpln><term lang="la">Capito, Cephalus
fluv</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="dace(n)">The <f type="i">Dace</f> or <f type="i">Dare</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Leuciscus</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="bleak(n)" lexeme="bley(n)">The <f type="i">Bleak</f> or <f type="i">Bley</f>: </form> <xpln><term lang="la">Alburnus.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="rudd(n)">The <f type="i">Roche</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Rubellio</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="gudgeon(n)">The <f type="i">Gudgeon</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Gobio fluviatilis</term>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="t" object="Fluviatiles l&aelig;ves"><alpha><term lang="la">Fluviatiles l&aelig;ves; </term><f type="i">River Fish
without scales</f>.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="eel(n)">The <f type="i">Eel</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Anguilla</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="eel-pout(n)" lexeme="burbot(n)">The <f type="i">Eel-pout</f> or <f type="i">Burbot</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Mustela</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="lampern(n)">The <f type="i">Lampern</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Lampetra minor</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="minnow(n)" lexeme="pink(n)">The <f type="i">Minow, Minim</f> or <f type="i">Pink</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Varius
sive Phoximus l&aelig;vis</term>.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="i1v">
<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="loach(n)">The <f type="i">Loche</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Cobites barbatula</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="stickleback(n)" lexeme="banstickle(n)">The <f type="i">Stickle-Back</f> or <f type="i">Banstickle</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Pun&shy;
gitius piscis</term>. Of this there are two kinds:
One that hath only three prickles on the
ridge of the back: another that hath six or
more.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lexeme="bullhead(n)" lexeme="miller's thumb(n)">The <f type="i">Bull-head</f> or <f type="i">Millers thumb</f>:</form> <xpln><term lang="la">Gobio
capitatus, Cottus</term> <f type="i">Rondel</f>.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>
